    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1624118899873661950">La villa est magnifique, l'été brûlant, la Méditerranée toute proche. Cécile a dix-sept ans. Elle ne connaît de l'amour que des baisers, des rendez-vous, des lassitudes. Pas pour longtemps. Son père, veuf, est un adepte joyeux des liaisons passagères et sans importance. Ils s'amusent, ils n'ont besoin de personne, ils sont heureux. La visite d'une femme de cœur, intelligen</span>
  <span id="freeText1624118899873661950" style="display:none">La villa est magnifique, l'été brûlant, la Méditerranée toute proche. Cécile a dix-sept ans. Elle ne connaît de l'amour que des baisers, des rendez-vous, des lassitudes. Pas pour longtemps. Son père, veuf, est un adepte joyeux des liaisons passagères et sans importance. Ils s'amusent, ils n'ont besoin de personne, ils sont heureux. La visite d'une femme de cœur, intelligente et calme, vient troubler ce délicieux désordre. Comment écarter la menace ? Dans la pinède embrasée, un jeu cruel se prépare.<br />C'était l'été 1954. On entendait pour la première fois la voix sèche et rapide d'un « charmant petit monstre » qui allait faire scandale. la deuxième moitié du XXe siècle commençait. Elle serait à l'image de cette adolescente déchirée entre le remords et le culte du plaisir.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1624118899873661950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

Do you think their marriage would've lasted? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/123353014-darkblueocean"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1603363079i/123353014._UY100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/123353014-darkblueocean">darkblueocean</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think he really loved her but i have to admit that I am also not sure if their marriage would have lasted.<br/>First Raymmond would not need to worry abo<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think he really loved her but i have to admit that I am also not sure if their marriage would have lasted.<br />First Raymmond would not need to worry about getting old alone. He has manny friends, love affairs and his daughter to spend time with. Of course that is not a typical way of spending your life as you grow up but this is also representing that new way of life and lifesyle sagan discribes in this novel. Thus he would not be alone when he gets old so from my point of view that would not be a reason for him to decide to marry Anne. <br />As I mentioned in the beginning I belive that he is honestly in love mith Anne eventhough she is different from the women he has been with before but that might exactly be why he likes her. But as Raymond has a rather impulsive, not to responsible way of life, I think you are right to question wheather this marriage would have lasted or not. Because Raymond might feel desire towards an other women wich when he would follow that desire might cause the end of that marriage because Anne expects it to be honest and responsible and not as impulsive and drawn to other love affairs like Raymonds life was before.<br />So to sum up I think Raymond honestly loves Anne but I share your concerns about the chances of that marriage to last. :))<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124965167" />

<span id="freeTextContainer8331850973404939311">503.	Bonjour Tristesse, Françoise Sagan<br/><br/>Bonjour Tristesse is a novel by Françoise Sagan. Published in 1954, when the author was only 18, it was an overnight sensation. An English-language film adaptation was released in 1958, directed by Otto Preminger. <br/><br/>17-year-old Cécile spends her summer in a villa on the French Riviera with her father Raymond and his current mistress, the young, superficial, fashionable Elsa, who gets on well with Cécile. <br/><br/>Raymond is an attractive, worldly, amoral man who excus</span>
  <span id="freeText8331850973404939311" style="display:none">503.	Bonjour Tristesse, Françoise Sagan<br /><br />Bonjour Tristesse is a novel by Françoise Sagan. Published in 1954, when the author was only 18, it was an overnight sensation. An English-language film adaptation was released in 1958, directed by Otto Preminger. <br /><br />17-year-old Cécile spends her summer in a villa on the French Riviera with her father Raymond and his current mistress, the young, superficial, fashionable Elsa, who gets on well with Cécile. <br /><br />Raymond is an attractive, worldly, amoral man who excuses his serial philandering with an Oscar Wilde quote about sin: <i>"Sin is the only note of vivid color that persists in the modern world."</i> <br /><br />Cécile says, <i>"I believed that I could base my life on it"</i>, and accepts their languorous lifestyle as the ideal of privileged status. <br /><br />One of its advantages for Cécile is that her father, who has no intellectual interests, does not care if she studies or not. Another is that he gives her leeway to pursue her own interests, with the assumption that she will be an amusing addition to the superficial social gatherings he favors. In the next villa to theirs is a young man in his 20's, Cyril, with whom Cécile has her first sexual romance. ...<br /><br />سلام بر غم - فرانسواز ساگان (هرم، فرخی) ادبیات فرانسه؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و پنجم ماه اکتبر سال 2008میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: سلام بر غم؛ نویسنده: فرانسواز ساگان؛ مترجم: حسینقلی جواهری؛ تهران، فرخی، چاپ سوم 1350 ، در 164ص؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، دنیای کتاب، 1396، در 178ص؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20م<br /><br />مترجم: فرزام حبیبی؛ تهران، هرم، 1385، در 164ص؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، زاویه، 1395؛ <br /><br />مترجم: علی اصغر محمدزاده؛ تهران، مرجان، 1380، در 160ص؛ شابک 9647563043؛<br /><br />مترجم: مریم احمدی، تهران، ایرون، 1381، در 191ص؛ شابک ایکس - 964202414؛<br /><br />به ندرت چنین کتابی خوانده ام؛ شاهکاری اسرارآمیز، که گشودن آن آسان نیست؛ خوانشگر همزمان احساس تنهایی می­کند و نمی­کند؛ «سلام بر غم»، نخستین رمان «فرانسواز ساگان» و یکی از معجزات نادر سده ی بگذشته (سده بیستم میلادی) است؛ در سال 1954میلادی دختری هجده ساله و نازپرورده؛ قلم به دست میگیرد؛ و مینگارد: <b>«تردید دارم، نام غم را به این احساس غریب و زیبا، که درد لطیفش آزارم میدهد، بدهم؛ چنان احساس منحصر به فرد و خودخواهانه ای ست، که تقریباً از آن خجالت میکشم؛ غم، همواره در نظرم، احساسی قابل احترام بوده، آن را نمیشناختم؛ اندوه، تاسف و گاهی ندامت را حس میکردم؛ حالا چیزی مانند ابریشم مرا دربر میگیرد، که نرم و خسته کننده است، و از دیگران جدایم میکند.»؛</b><br /><br />نخستین بخش از کتاب «ساگان»، افسون موسیقی، و غم، نویسنده اش را دربر دارد؛ او داستان «سسیل» را بازگو میکند؛ کودک بدبختی از یک خانواده ثروتمند، که مشغول سپری کردن تعطیلات خود، با پدر دیوانه، و معشوقه ی اوست؛ همه چیز به خوبی در یک فضای زنده و آرام میگذرد؛ تا روزیکه پدر تصمیم میگیرد، با معشوقه ی خود «آنا»، که نسبتاً جدی و متعادل است، و امکان دارد این زندگی بی دغدغه را از بین ببرد، ازدواج کند؛ «سسیل» تصمیم میگیرد، نقشه ی آنها را برهم بریزد؛ پیروز میشود، و پایان این شوخی، یک ماجرای غم انگیز است؛ «ساگان» در سال 1954میلادی و در هجده سالگی، با نوشتن همین داستان «سلام بر غم»، به جاودانگی رسید؛ داستان به بیش از بیست زبان ترجمه و برگردانده شد؛ سرگذشت «سسیل» دختر هفده ساله ی یک خانواده ثروتمند، که تنها به لذتهای زندگی توجه دارد، و از تنهایی، به طرح توطئه ی قتل میپردازد؛ «ساگان» چهل داستان و نمایشنامه های دیگر هم بنوشته است، که عنوانهاشان: «لبخند»، «بازهم خداحافظ»، «چشمهای ابریشمی»، «رختخوابی که جمع نمیشد»، «حافظ قلب»، «یک روستا در سوئد»، و بالاخره «اتوبیوگرافی» ...؛<br /><br />ایشان در زمینه اندیشه ی فلسفی نیز، نکات جالبی از خویش به یادگار بگذاشته اند، از جمله این نکته و جمله ی نغز که: «قوانین برای انطباق با خواست مردم، و تحقق آن، باید طرح و تصویب شوند؛ نه با این هدف دولت، که مردمان بر خلاف میل و نیازشان، پس از تصویب، و آغاز اجرای قانون موضوعه، خود را با آن قوانین وفق و تطبیق دهند». پایان نقل<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 30/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="8331850973404939311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Cue never-ending angst, alcohol, scheming, glam life, underage sex, and boredom, boredom, boredom, and voila! - a bestseller and a classic.<br/><br/>Please, somebody, get her to do some chores so that she doesn't stuff her head with rubbish!<br/><br/>I am tired of such tripe being praised because of <b>"but the author was only 17 when s</b></span>
  <span id="freeText6396359133664778920" style="display:none">This is why I don't read books written by teenagers. <span id="freeTextContainer3946135084927316557">The French seventeen year-old girl narrator is at once astute AND childish. The dolce vita of youth and leisure is intoxicating, to partake in or merely read about, and her bohemian experiences and attitudes give the work a refreshing polish. This is somewhat of the opposite of "Catcher in the Rye" since Cecile is experienced, active, cool, while Holden Caulfield. has all the naivete of a newborn, is more of a spectator & is simply, when all is said & done, just duh-hull.</span>
  <span id="freeText3946135084927316557" style="display:none">The French seventeen year-old girl narrator is at once astute AND childish. <div id="review_2828892000" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2828892000" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2828892000?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2828892000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12743838249980828824">Françoise Sagan's amoral novel of a schoolgirl's summer romance, scandalised French society at the time, and, in the process, catapulted her into the limelight, at age only eighteen. Not that it really did her any good, being blighted by drink, drugs and unhappy relationships thereafter.<br/><br/>The narrative is told by seventeen-year-old Cécile, holidaying on the Côte d'Azur with her widowed father, a roué who has brought along his young girlfriend. The daughter is exploring her own first sentimental an</span>
  <span id="freeText12743838249980828824" style="display:none">Françoise Sagan's amoral novel of a schoolgirl's summer romance, scandalised French society at the time, and, in the process, catapulted her into the limelight, at age only eighteen. Not that it really did her any good, being blighted by drink, drugs and unhappy relationships thereafter.<br /><br />The narrative is told by seventeen-year-old Cécile, holidaying on the Côte d'Azur with her widowed father, a roué who has brought along his young girlfriend. The daughter is exploring her own first sentimental and sensual adventure, a swiftly consummated romance with a handsome law student, when the unexpected arrival of an older woman, a friend of her late mother, disrupts the self-indulgent haze of high summer. First the newcomer takes charge, ordering Cécile to terminate her romance in order to stay indoors and do her homework. Then she and the father fall in love. To prevent their marriage the daughter devises an ill-fated plot in which the pretence of an affair between her boyfriend and the father's dumped girlfriend is intended to provoke jealousy and restore the status quo.<br /><br />The amorality and sensualism of the characters seem less shocking today, but on the whole the book won me over for two reasons - it's matter-of-fact, existential style that evoked Albert Camus, and the setting - the sun and the sea of the French Riviera, plus of course there's the sex, the gambling, and riding about in fast automobiles. Bonjour Tristesse stands today as a fascinating look at a France that no longer exists. It is an invocation of an era, of a time when young people were beginning to seek freedom from the strict bourgeois society of France after the end of WW2.<br /><br />Loved the movie as well, probably just as much as the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12743838249980828824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169783260" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169783260" />
    <a title="Megha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1727205-megha"><img alt="Megha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361664925p2/1727205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169783260?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megha" href="/user/show/1727205-megha">Megha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7797703-bonjour-tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1727205-megha?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169783260" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17883715066895986861">Being stuck on the runway for three hours with <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> in hand is no fun, I tell you.<br/><br/>I read this at a time when I had a lot on my plate. I didn't have enough patience to be concerned about the problems of a bunch of vain people who are wealthier than Scrooge McDuck, who spend their days sun-bathing and surfing and whose evenings are dedicated to drinking and dancing.<br/><br/>Yeah, I agree it is well-written for an 18 year old author. But it is also so superficial. It is natural that the 17 year</span>
  <span id="freeText17883715066895986861" style="display:none">Being stuck on the runway for three hours with <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> in hand is no fun, I tell you.<br /><br />I read this at a time when I had a lot on my plate. I didn't have enough patience to be concerned about the problems of a bunch of vain people who are wealthier than Scrooge McDuck, who spend their days sun-bathing and surfing and whose evenings are dedicated to drinking and dancing.<br /><br />Yeah, I agree it is well-written for an 18 year old author. But it is also so superficial. It is natural that the 17 year old narrator acts like a teenager. What is extremely annoying is that all the adults behave like teenagers as well. The average IQ of all the characters in this book is likely to be very low. They simply blame the heat for all their stupidity.<br /><br />Let's meet these characters:<br /><br /><b>Cecile:</b> spoiled daughter of a rich dad. Her life sucks right now, because her dad's girlfriend is trying to make her study for an exam and doesn't let her sleep with the pretty boy. Could it be any worse!<br /><br /><b>Raymond:</b> rich, irresponsible, promiscuous. <br />Hey, Raymond, you have a daughter to look after, remember?<br /><br /><b>Elsa:</b> beautiful, fashionable, DUMB.<br />Occupation: being a mistress to rich men.<br /><br /><b>Cyril:</b> 25 year old boy that Cecile finds attractive. Considering how he gets involved into Cecile's schemes, probably not too bright.<br /><br /><b>Anne:</b> cold, calculating, condescending. She decided she wanted Raymond in her life, followed him down to his beach-house, wore a flattering dress one evening and voila! mission accomplished.<br /><br /><br />**** HERE BE A MAJOR SPOILER. LOOK AWAY. ****<br /><br />I do have sympathy for Anne's tragic end, but there is this another voice in my head that wants to shout at Anne: <i>What are you, a silly teenager?!</i> A woman her age should know better than to end her life over a short relationship with a frivolous man known to be loose with women. Anne's action is also difficult to digest as there was no indication of love or any real emotion in their relationship. The two barely even spoke.<br />Damn, this heat. Look where it got her!<br /><br />****<br /><br />I spent the rest of the flight reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49552.The_Stranger" title="The Stranger by Albert Camus" rel="nofollow noopener">The Stranger</a> which, like this one, featured French people who spent a lot of time on the beach and cursed the heat. But then there was so much more too. Way better!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17883715066895986861" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_826682601" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/826682601" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/826682601?book_show_action=true">Jan 17, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780241951569" href="/book/show/290897.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who thrive in sadness</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      Emilie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer826682601" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7240481602374562188"><i>“Adieu tristesse,<br/>Bonjour tristesse.<br/>Tu es inscrite dans les lignes du plafond.<br/>Tu es inscrite dans les yeux que j’aime<br/>Tu n’es pas tout à fait la misère,<br/>Car les lèvres les plus pauvres te dénoncent<br/>Par un sourire.<br/>Bonjour tristesse.<br/>Amour des corps aimables.<br/>Puissance de l’amour<br/>Dont l’amabilité surgit<br/>Comme un monstre sans corps.<br/>Tête désappointée.<br/>Tristesse, beau visage.”</i><br/><b>Paul Éluard</b>, <i>“À Peine Défigurée”</i><br/><br/><i> “Adieu Tristesse<br/>Bonjour Tristesse<br/>Farewell Sadness<br/>Hello Sadness<br/>You are inscribed in the lines on the ce</i></span>
  <span id="freeText7240481602374562188" style="display:none"><i>“Adieu tristesse,<br />Bonjour tristesse.<br />Tu es inscrite dans les lignes du plafond.<br />Tu es inscrite dans les yeux que j’aime<br />Tu n’es pas tout à fait la misère,<br />Car les lèvres les plus pauvres te dénoncent<br />Par un sourire.<br />Bonjour tristesse.<br />Amour des corps aimables.<br />Puissance de l’amour<br />Dont l’amabilité surgit<br />Comme un monstre sans corps.<br />Tête désappointée.<br />Tristesse, beau visage.”</i><br /><b>Paul Éluard</b>, <i>“À Peine Défigurée”</i><br /><br /><i> “Adieu Tristesse<br />Bonjour Tristesse<br />Farewell Sadness<br />Hello Sadness<br />You are inscribed in the lines on the ceiling<br />You are inscribed in the eyes that I love<br />You are not poverty absolutely<br />Since the poorest of lips denounce you<br />Ah with a smile<br />Bonjour Tristesse<br />Love of kind bodies<br />Power of love<br />From which kindness rises<br />Like a bodiless monster<br />Unattached head<br />Sadness beautiful face.”</i><br /><b>Paul Éluard</b>, <i>“Barely Disfigured”</i><br /><br />As in Paul Éluard’s poem, sadness and foreboding soak this short narrative tingeing it with subtle melancholy disguised in frivolous characters and superficial undertone. The irredeemable burden of selfishness, the strain of a guilty conscience, the disillusionment of unveiled truth, the weight of wrong decisions taken on impulse impregnate the voice of capricious and pampered Cécile, a precocious seventeen year-old girl , who discloses a confession rather than the memories of a summer spent in a paradisiacal villa in the south of France. Céline’s charming and irresponsible father Raymond treats her with the courtesy and tenderness more becoming of a lover than a paternal figure and his refusal of all notions of fidelity and serious commitment defines Cécile’s approach to relationships as passing, rapid, violent and passionate affairs as well as a lifestyle full of free love, lavish luxury, debauchery and hedonistic pleasures. <br /><br /><i> “Although I didn’t share my father’s aversion to ugliness, which often led us to associate with stupid people, I felt vaguely uncomfortable with anyone devoid of physical charms.” (p.5)</i><br /><br />None of her father’s numerous and shallow mistresses has ever threatened to disrupt Céline and Raymond’s unshakable duo until the arrival of Anne, a perspicacious and discerning old friend of Cécile’s deceased mother. Anne’s more traditional, serious and sensible conduct, which represents the conventional idea of love, marriage and responsibility that Céline so much detests, jeopardizes her precious freedom and carefree existence when her father unexpectedly announces his intention of marrying her. <br />Blinded by her self-interests and unconscious jealousy of Anne for banishing her from being the apple of Raymond’s eye, Cécile starts plotting a plan with Cyril, his young and golden skinned summer lover, to recover her former bourgeoisie and unorthodox life with her father.<br /><br /><i> “I feared boredom and tranquility more than anything. In order to achieve serenity, my father and I had to have excitement, and this Anne was not prepared to admit.” (p.82)</i><br /><br /><i> Bonjour Tristesse </i> was published in 1954, when Françoise Sagan was barely eighteen years old after having failed her foundation-year examinations at the Sorbonne. She replaced her original surname by a <i>nom de plume</i> taken from Proust’s character the <i>Princesse de Sagan</i> and her rebellious coming-of-age novel became both a huge success and a scandal for the underlying skepticism regarding conventional institutions like marriage and family as well as for the subtle hint of a disturbing incestuous nature of father-daughter relationship that impregnates the story. <br /><br />Sagan soon became representative of the bored and disillusioned young generation whose main focus was a superfluous existence immersed in self-indulgence and decadent pleasure. But both the confessional timbre and the satirical tone of Sagan’s voice reverberating in a controlled, even austere writing style, which doesn’t succumb to lyricism or redundant literary ornaments, shows me otherwise. There is sadness in lonely people trying to fill their artificial relationships with glib gratification. There is sadness in daily conversations revealing the meaninglessness of a life dissipated, with no clear direction. There is sadness in evoking a decisive moment lost in time, when silence became too heavy of a burden to carry and the sound of fear overcame the music of righteousness, creating a dissonant path of no return. There is sadness in shame and remorse. There is sadness in a sense of loss.<br /><br /><i>Oscar Wilde</i> implied deceit inherently thrives in modern times: “<i> “Sin is the only note of vivid color that persists in the modern world.”</i> Both Céline and Sagan knew decisions can’t be unmade and that they come with burdensome and sometimes unpardonable consequences. One can be courageous and face past transgressions with integrity or elude guilt and create a groundless existence based on self-deception and forgetfulness while being condemned to never get rid of the unbearable lightness of perennial sadness. Reality might prove to be as complex as human beings are ignorant, but Céline’s preference to tread the second path proved such an anticlimax that I couldn’t get over the irony of choosing sadness to avoid remorse while bearing the ignominy of it all with a distorted smile plastered on Céline's pretty and tanned face.<br /><br /><i> “A strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sadness.” (p. 3) </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7240481602374562188" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2410572378" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2410572378" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55478778-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462205788p2/55478778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2410572378?book_show_action=true">Jun 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/55478778-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140011920" href="/book/show/1183169.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=women-european">women-european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55478778-laura?shelf=20th-century-women">20th-century-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2410572378" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12127791620944051583">I enjoyed this very much. I read it when I was a teenager - I remember devouring all the bits about love and sex etc - basically being jealous of Cécile - so much happening to her and she is only 17! And now reading it again, aged 53 - I still love it. The psychology rings true and is in fact quite remarkable, as Sagan wrote this - her first book at the age of 18. After failing her exams to continue at the Sorbonne - she took herself off to the family's villa in the south of France and wrote the</span>
  <span id="freeText12127791620944051583" style="display:none">I enjoyed this very much. I read it when I was a teenager - I remember devouring all the bits about love and sex etc - basically being jealous of Cécile - so much happening to her and she is only 17! And now reading it again, aged 53 - I still love it. The psychology rings true and is in fact quite remarkable, as Sagan wrote this - her first book at the age of 18. After failing her exams to continue at the Sorbonne - she took herself off to the family's villa in the south of France and wrote the book there. If you know the story - then these themes are part of Cécile's life - her soon to be stepmother Anne is adamant that she must study for her entrance exams - to university.<br /><br />The only point where I felt - as in several other books - I have the same complaint - the ending, the drama of the ending is hyped beyond what is absolutely necessary. It would have been more than enough for Anne to drive off and leave the self-indulgent pair - father and daughter, Raymond and Cécile to their decadent life-style.<br /><br />What elevates this book, I think, is Cécile's scrupulous self-assessment in relation to the other characters. She knows for example that Anne would be good for her. She has already stated that her father taking her to his parties in Paris and disappearing with an attractive girl for the night - is not the norm. Cécile has also noted the looks when she is with her father, a 17 year-old with a 40 year old and understood that those sly looks means they are perceived as a couple - icky !<br /><br />This book was written in 1954 - and is remarkably open in its sexually liberated discussions between father and daughter. Cécile is fully aware of her father's sex-life but also knows that the shortness of his attachments is compensated by his kindness and affection - towards his mistresses. I liked this analysis - I am continually surprised actually that an eighteen year old could write such psychologically complete characters. The difference in myself reading it as a teenager compared with now is quite clear to me. I know for certain that I was drawn in by the broad strokes of the novel's plot - that I fully understood Cécile's anguish at being locked in her room, in being shocked and devastated when she is banned from seeing Cyril and yet her father who has previously given her so much freedom does nothing to intervene when his new partner Anne decides that she is now responsible for the upbringing and welfare of Cécile.<br /><br />As a mature reader, however, I can see all the subtleties. How Cécile is conflicted, how she is tortured by her plot against Anne taking off under its own steam, how she feels that she is absolved from the consequences of her interference; she asserts that her father is an adult, responsible for his own actions and decisions and yet she knows that it is her close understanding of his character that has allowed her plot to develop in the way it does. <br /><br />In fact the more I think about this book, the more astonished I am at how the wrench and conflict of emotions is betrayed so clearly in such a simple style and in basically a very simple story. <br /><br />I think there are some strong anthropological issues being presented here - for example Anne wins Raymond's heart, expunges his mistress Elsa from the villa and enjoys a sexually fulfilling and deeply satisfying relationship with him all in front of the eyes of the seventeen year old AND at the same time bans Cécile from exploring her own sexuality with the attractive, young man from next door. Surely this is not fair? There are strong ties and boundaries being displaced and evolved here - the missing mother, sexual freedom for who? disruption of the tie between father and daughter and yet Anne is both right and undeniably wrong.<br /><br />There are probably only a rare handful of books which could hold my attention as both a teenager and an adult - kudos to this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12127791620944051583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_965183000" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/965183000" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/965183000?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141032917" href="/book/show/1183167.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=to-buy-physical-copy">to-buy-physical-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=first-novels">first-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=read-on-kindle">read-on-kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=1950s-release">1950s-release</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer965183000" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3061613525969756567">This short and sparkling novel was famously published when the author was just 18 years old. While the same length as some short stories, <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> feels fully-formed and deftly plotted. The narrator, Cécile, is a 17-year-old girl enjoying an extended summer holiday in the south of France with her father. Cécile is pampered, spoilt and somewhat bratty; her father, who she worships, is a louche and charming womaniser. They see themselves as free spirits, although their 'easy' lifestyle is</span>
  <span id="freeText3061613525969756567" style="display:none">This short and sparkling novel was famously published when the author was just 18 years old. While the same length as some short stories, <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> feels fully-formed and deftly plotted. The narrator, Cécile, is a 17-year-old girl enjoying an extended summer holiday in the south of France with her father. Cécile is pampered, spoilt and somewhat bratty; her father, who she worships, is a louche and charming womaniser. They see themselves as free spirits, although their 'easy' lifestyle is, of course, only enabled by their significant wealth. At the beginning of the book they are staying in a villa along with Daddy's current mistress, Elsa (more than a decade his junior), and doing little other than sunbathing, swimming and socialising. Cécile is also conducting a half-hearted (on her side) romance with a young man, Cyril, who is staying in the same resort. This dreamy equilibrium is disturbed when her father invites a female acquaintance of his own age - Anne, best friend of Cécile's late mother - to join the trip. Anne's presence provokes Cécile's jealousy and uspets the balance of her father's relationship with Elsa, leading our narrator to devise a deadly plan.<br /><br />The narrative captures the melodrama and insouciance of teenage years so well. I'm tempted to say it's incredible that the author managed this when she was still so young herself, but maybe this is the sort of insight you could only have while still living the experience? In any case, Cécile is an incredibly well-drawn teenager and her characterisation displays a remarkable self-awareness on Sagan's part. I was drawn back into that strange mix of overreacting to the smallest things while at the same time disregarding some of the biggest; Cécile's father's romance with Anne is the end of the world, and Anne's insistence that she should study for exams nothing short of a life-ruining catastrophe, but Cyril's apparent love for her is just a game, one she is not prepared to indulge fully because he likes her <i>too much</i>. Cécile may be selfish and petulant, but her naivety allows for insights the adults sometimes miss. About her father's engagement, for example, 'I had so often seen him happy on account of a woman' - well, indeed! There is also, sometimes, a sense that the novel is written from a future standpoint, that of a mature woman reflecting on her youth. Perhaps this, too, is typical of the character/author's age: when you're 18, the stuff you did when you were 17 seems a million miles away.<br /><br />With a precocious teenage protagonist, over-privileged rich people swanning around doing whatever they want, and a 26-year-old man 'falling in love' with a girl who isn't even out of school, you'd think I would have absolutely hated this. In fact I thought it was utterly brilliant; I can't find anything critical to say about it at all. I should have read <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> a long time ago, and I heartily recommend it to anyone who hasn't - it only takes about an hour to get through and it's well worth such a small portion of your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3061613525969756567" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13267747976872939561">So what to say about Cecile and her incessant scheming? Apparently a summer on the Med, smoking and drinking on daddy's dollar (or franc) is not enough for well bred young ladies these days. Where swimming, sunbathing and generally being a bright young thing were once enough, Cecile ups the ante and decides that a more diverting way to spend the summer is to plot the downfall of her fathers current relationship and in between times, try to loose her virginity to the likeable but none too bright </span>
  <span id="freeText13267747976872939561" style="display:none">So what to say about Cecile and her incessant scheming? Apparently a summer on the Med, smoking and drinking on daddy's dollar (or franc) is not enough for well bred young ladies these days. Where swimming, sunbathing and generally being a bright young thing were once enough, Cecile ups the ante and decides that a more diverting way to spend the summer is to plot the downfall of her fathers current relationship and in between times, try to loose her virginity to the likeable but none too bright older man with the boat.A girl with undoubtedly cruel intentions (and probably a battered and dog-eared copy of Les Liasons Dangereuse by Laclos tucked under her mattress). Unfortunately all the drinking, smoking and generally being louche don't actually cancel out a moral conscience and before she knows it her frankly childish meddlings are moving at a pace she can no longer control and she veers between desperate guilt and ambivalence as events unfold. Ultimately the characters offer you very little to love about them - Cecile is naieve and manipulative, Anne is a control freak in a Chanel twin-piece and dear old daddy is an aging play boy with a slightly disturbing penchant for girls much closer to his daughters age than his own.<br /><br />Francoise Sagan wrote this book at the age of 18 then presumably sat down, lit a cigarette and pouted over how to spend the next 60 years given that she'd already written her great masterpiece which clearly extols her youthful genius. <br /><br />Youthful or not,it is a great read, and I liked it a lot. It's tightly controlled and well put together; a master class in how to be a moody teen but with the classic teen scent of festering bedroom cancelled out by the faint eau de nil of youthful genius. </span>

<div id="review_416535877" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/416535877" />
    <a title="Madeline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111921-madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/416535877?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeline" href="/user/show/111921-madeline">Madeline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=the-list">the-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer416535877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16586176120141003230">"That summer, I was seventeen and perfectly happy. ...My father was forty, and had been a widower for fifteen years. He was young for his age, full of vitality and liveliness. When I left my convent school two years before and came to Paris to live with him, I soon realized that he was living with a woman. But I was slower in accepting the fact that his fancy changed every six months! But gradually his charm, my new easy life, and my own disposition, led me to fall in readily with his ways. He w</span>
  <span id="freeText16586176120141003230" style="display:none">"That summer, I was seventeen and perfectly happy. ...My father was forty, and had been a widower for fifteen years. He was young for his age, full of vitality and liveliness. When I left my convent school two years before and came to Paris to live with him, I soon realized that he was living with a woman. But I was slower in accepting the fact that his fancy changed every six months! But gradually his charm, my new easy life, and my own disposition, led me to fall in readily with his ways. He was a frivolous man, clever at business, always curious, quickly bored, and very attractive to women. It was easy for me to love him, for he was kind, generous, gay, and fond of me. I cannot imagine a better or more amusing companion."<br /><br />This lovely little novel, deceptively slim, made me want to read it while lying on a beach. It's full of rich French people being delightfully and almost stereotypically French at their villa in the Mediterranean, and all the romantic drama and emotional backstabbing that occurs there. The narrator, Cecile, is enjoying her hedonistic lifestyle when her playboy father announces, unexpectedly, that he is getting married. The woman in question is Anne, a family friend who is the opposite of the previous mistresses Cecile's father has had: she's elegant, poised, practical, intelligent, dignified, and forty-two. Cecile recognizes the threat that Anne poses to her carefree life, and decides to destroy the relationship:<br /><br />"She would gradually turn us into the husband and step-daughter of Anne Larsen, that is to say, she would turn us into two civilized, well-behaved and contented persons. For she would certainly be good to us. How easily - unstable and irresponsible as we were - we would yield to her influence, and be fitted into the attractive framework of her orderly plan of living. She was much too efficient. Already my father was separated from me. I was hurt by his embarrassed face, turning away from me at the table. Tears came to my eyes at the thought of the jokes we used to have together, our gay laughter as we drove home at dawn through the deserted streets of Paris. All that was over. In my turn I would be influenced, readjusted, remodeled by Anne. I would not even mind it, she would handle me with such intelligence, humor, and sweetness. I wouldn't be able to resist her. In six months I should no longer even want to."<br /><br />In another writer's hands, this story could have gone horribly wrong - Cecile has every opportunity to turn into a spoiled rich brat who can't stand the idea of being forced to behave like an adult with responsibilities, and the way she tries to destroy her father's happiness could be seen as the actions of a borderline-psychotic. The genius of Sagan's book is that she doesn't try to justify Cecile's actions. We see the horrible truth of what Cecile is doing, and <i>so does Cecile</i>. Every few chapters (sometimes every few pages) Cecile will have a moment of clarity, and realize that Anne is a good person and that her father is happy, and she regrets her meddling. But then she goes right back to her destructive plan, because she can't help herself. By letting us see Cecile wrestling with her own conscience, and ultimately being unable to resist her destructive urge, Sagan creates one of the best portrayals of a teenage girl I've ever read. <br /><br />"Although I did not share my father's intense aversion to ugliness - which often led us to associate with stupid people - I did feel vaguely uncomfortable in the presence of anyone completely devoid of physical charm. Their resignation to the fact that they were unattractive seemed to me somehow indecent. For what are we looking for if not to please? I do not know if the desire to attract others comes from a superabundance of vitality, possessiveness, or the hidden, unspoken need to be reassured."</span>
  <a data-text-id="16586176120141003230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review416535877">

<div id="review_773220855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/773220855" />
    <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca"><img alt="Jim Fonseca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1397669173p2/6743601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/773220855?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Fonseca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Fonseca" href="/user/show/6743601-jim-fonseca">Jim Fonseca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6743601-jim-fonseca?shelf=french-authors">french-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer773220855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8919007346272614076">A seventeen year old girl and her father are stumbling through life after the death of her mother, his wife. The father is a poster child for poor parenting; he brings a series of women into the home and takes his daughter to parties and casinos where she interacts with much older men, dancing, drinking and smoking. (After all, this is France.) Finally he may settle down and marry one of the women but the daughter, dreading rules and regulations, bed-time and study hours, spins a web of intrigue</span>
  <span id="freeText8919007346272614076" style="display:none">A seventeen year old girl and her father are stumbling through life after the death of her mother, his wife. The father is a poster child for poor parenting; he brings a series of women into the home and takes his daughter to parties and casinos where she interacts with much older men, dancing, drinking and smoking. (After all, this is France.) Finally he may settle down and marry one of the women but the daughter, dreading rules and regulations, bed-time and study hours, spins a web of intrigue around them. The result? Well, let’s remember the title translates as “Hello Sadness.” (The book is translated from the French.) As I was reading this very short book (you can read its 127 pages in one sitting) I occasionally thought that it was a bit of stretch to believe that a seventeen-year-old girl could philosophize this deeply about love, life and men, and then I read that the author was nineteen when she wrote this book. Amazing!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8919007346272614076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53025675?book_show_action=true">Apr 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">francophiles, bookish teen girls and former bookish teen girls</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=20th-century-postwar-to-late">20th-century-postwar-to-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=tres-francais">tres-francais</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53025675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4806393105019281111">A lovely pyschological gem that seems to perfectly depict what it was like to be smart, rich and seventeen years old in 1954. I particularly liked the delicate, exquisite, admirably honest rendering of Cecile's emotions, whether ugly or sweet. I was that age not so very long ago- it isn't hard to remember the truth in what she's saying here.<br/><br/>Should be read in one sitting- with tea on an unhurried Sunday afternoon.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_967911504" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/967911504" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/967911504?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140278781" href="/book/show/805039.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=french-novels">french-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer967911504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17805793161090370291">Written when Sagan was still a teenager it is the story of Cecile, a seventeen year old girl who lives with her amoral and dissolute father who has a different woman in tow every two months or so. This year seems to be the year for vapid teenagers; having read A Clockwork Orange and The Catcher in the Rye. Admittedly this was better than the latter and at least here there is some self knowledge and development over the period of the book.<br/>There are few players. Cecile is 17, rich, spoilt and supp</span>
  <span id="freeText17805793161090370291" style="display:none">Written when Sagan was still a teenager it is the story of Cecile, a seventeen year old girl who lives with her amoral and dissolute father who has a different woman in tow every two months or so. This year seems to be the year for vapid teenagers; having read A Clockwork Orange and The Catcher in the Rye. Admittedly this was better than the latter and at least here there is some self knowledge and development over the period of the book.<br />There are few players. Cecile is 17, rich, spoilt and supposed to be studying for university over the summer. She is staying for the summer in a villa in the South of France near the beach; there is no studying. Raymond, her father, widowed many years ago has a series of much younger lovers who each last a couple of months or so. Cecile is lacking a mother figure; she is also, it might be argued lacking a father figure as well! Elsa is Raymond’s latest love interest; 29 and red haired with a tendency to sunburn which makes her a figure of ridicule for Cecile. Cyril is an older man with a boat (only 26, but at 17 that is ancient!) who is interested in Cecile; a potential lover. Anne is a sophisticated woman in her early 40s, an acquaintance of Raymond and friend of his late wife, who comes to stay and displaces the young lover. Raymond and she decide to marry and Cecile sees her idyllic lifestyle about to disappear. Anne treats Cecile as a child and expects her to actually do some studying. Cecile resents her and hatches a plan with unforeseen consequences.<br />Most of the characters are immature; adolescent even and the only person who behaves like an adult in the book (Anne) is the centre of resentment. This may be because it was written by a teenager. Cecile appears to be looking for a parent, but the reality of an adult creating boundaries leaves her cold. <br />It’s an easy read but ultimately the troubles of the idle rich are rather boring and predictable and it was difficult to have real sympathy for any of the characters.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17805793161090370291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1684649239" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1684649239" />
    <a title="Aditi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16069171-aditi"><img alt="Aditi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480260251p2/16069171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1684649239?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aditi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aditi" href="/user/show/16069171-aditi">Aditi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780241951569" href="/book/show/290897.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=friends">friends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=my-reviews">my-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=family">family</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16069171-aditi?shelf=penguin-books">penguin-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1684649239" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16861479806614320433"> <i>"L’amour fait les plus grandes douceurs et les plus sensibles infortunes de la vie."</i><br/><br/>English translation: <i>"Love makes the greatest pleasures and most sensitive misfortunes of life."</i><br/><br/> ----Madeleine de Scudery<br/><br/><br/>Françoise Sagan, an award-winning French novelist, has created an uproar in the French literary world as well as in the French community, with her debut novel, <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> that has been translated into English by Irene Ash, after its crazy popularity and scandal, that earned Sagan a repu</span>
  <span id="freeText16861479806614320433" style="display:none"> <i>"L’amour fait les plus grandes douceurs et les plus sensibles infortunes de la vie."</i><br /><br />English translation: <i>"Love makes the greatest pleasures and most sensitive misfortunes of life."</i><br /><br /> ----Madeleine de Scudery<br /><br /><br />Françoise Sagan, an award-winning French novelist, has created an uproar in the French literary world as well as in the French community, with her debut novel, <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> that has been translated into English by Irene Ash, after its crazy popularity and scandal, that earned Sagan a reputable spot in the literary world. Sagan was barely eighteen when she published her first novel featuring a teenage girl flying away freely with love, physical intimacy, carelessness, promiscuity and other such immoral acts, on the pretext of her father's engagement with rather young mistresses. <br /><br /><br /><b>Synopsis: </b><br /><br /><i>'Late into the night we talked of love, of its complications. In my father's eyes they were imaginary. . . This conception of rapid, violent and passing love affairs appealed to my imagination. I was not at the age when fidelity is attractive. I knew very little about love.'<br /><br />The French Riviera: home to the Beautiful People. And none are more beautiful than Cécile, a precocious seventeen-year-old, and her father Raymond, a vivacious libertine. Charming, decadent and irresponsible, the golden-skinned duo are dedicated to a life of free love, fast cars and hedonistic pleasures. But then, one long, hot summer Raymond decides to marry, and Cécile and her lover Cyril feel compelled to take a hand in his amours, with tragic consequences.<br /><br />Bonjour Tristesse scandalized 1950s France with its portrayal of teenager terrible Cécile, a heroine who rejects conventional notions of love, marriage and responsibility to choose her own sexual freedom. </i><br /><br /><br />Cécile, a 17-year old unstable teenager, who after failing her exams, goes on a lavish Mediterranean vacation trip to Côte d'Azur with her equally reckless, widowed and <i>Don Juan</i>kind father and his young mistress, Elsa. Cécile soon jumps into a summer romance with an older yet handsome man, Cyril, who loved her madly. Although, Cécile confessed that she was never in love with him, only with the idea of physical intimacy, care and warmth shared by that man, while her father was busy with the young woman. Cécile's carefree summer days are soon disrupted by the arrival of her deceased mother's long time friend, Anne, who is a woman of class with education, self-respect and her own independence that rather intimidated the young girl, Cécile. Anne soon launches herself up to mother and take care of Cécile's future and her education by putting an end to the whirlwind summer romance with Cyril. And within few days, Cécile's nightmares turn out to be true when her father announces that he will marry Anne as he is madly in love with her. Unable to take the news and the thought of Anne disrupting the slightly carnal relationship between her father and herself, she decides to tear Anne's relationship with her father by using Cyril and Elsa's false love affair to invoke jealousy in her father. But does she succeed? <br /><br />This is not a joy ride to read this overripe account of a bored and very, very dull 17-year old teenage girl who is a rich, spoilt brat, but think again, this has been written by someone who is of same age as the protagonist, so you can't expect any literary creativity from the young author. <i>So how did the novel create so much noise in the literary world? Very simple answer</i>, mainly because of its unconventional approach and nature of a French teenage girl's lifestyle that broke all the social norms thus making it s popular book. Yeah something like <i>Lolita</i> by Vladimir Nabokov. <br /><br /><i>So why bore oneself by reading about a barely eighteen year old's carnal and jealous desires?</i> If you have wasted your money on buying this book, otherwise, there is no such major reason to read this extremely stupid book. <i>Okay, let's no be harsh here, I mean can you imagine yourself in penning a classic literary masterpiece at the age of 18? </i> If the answer is no, then find some time to get your hands on this book to imagine that!<br /><br />The writing style is not that polished, yet very articulate that readers can easily comprehend with. The narrative is quite dull and at times bit baffling with the complexity involved in the dialogues. The story is narrated from a 17 year old teenager's voice and through her perspective, the readers are forced to form an opinion on other characters in the book. The pacing is really fast and over a large tumbler of hot coffee, this can be easily read in just one sitting. The story flows freely as Cécile jumps from one hedonistic act to another and the readers, here, might feel arrested to her life story. <br /><br />The French backdrop is extremely alluring and vividly painted into the canvas of this story, where the author has captured a France's glamorous beach villa, the lifestyle of the posh, aristocrat and rich French folks, the then culture as well as the sandy blue and golden landscapes that will instantly transport the readers to this exotic and filthy rich destination where these days the who's who of A-class Hollywood celebrities to world class politicians take a vacation. <br /><br />The characters in this book are very stereotypical and extremely unreal. And also they are not that well-developed with their flaws or multiple layers, instead they are very much one-dimensional, and in word, each and every character is witless like the main character, Cécile, is witless enough to scheme a devious plan to put an end to her father's relationship with the woman who simply asked her to study instead of wasting her time with Cyril, Anne is witless enough to jump into some life changing decisions, Cyril is witless enough to fall for Cécile's stupid plan, Elsa is extremely witless and has no intelligence or never uses her own brain as she lives on the money provided to her by rich and old French men, and Raymond, Cécile's father is another witless man who has forgotten that he even has a daughter as he is so busy in frivolities with silly women. <br /><br />In a nutshell, the story is slightly compelling enough to keep the readers engaged and the book definitely deserves a 3-star rating mainly because this piece of fiction has been produced by an eighteen year old young lady whose life drastically changes after selling her debut book, let's just say her life became a train of unhappy events after another, except money that never left her side, drowning her into the dark world of gambling, fast cars, alcohol, drugs, lavish mansion and lots of love affairs both with men and women. <br /><br /><b>Verdict: </b> <i>A sardonic tale that breaks the conventional norms for a young woman in a French society. </i><br /><br /><b>Courtesy: </b> <i>Thanks to the publishers from Penguin India for giving me the opportunty to read and review this book. </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16861479806614320433" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3347602357" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3347602357" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48011083-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542289434p2/48011083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3347602357?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/48011083-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3347602357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1221226580585201745"><i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> is an engaging novella. In rather short order, I found sharp contempt for Céline, the main character, a destructress and a reminder of our potential to inflict emotional cruelty amid betrayal, even to our closest family. I imagine a Sisyphean dilemma; by nature, we are communal, subject to a spectrum of miseries in our nearest relationships; yet the antidote, solitude, seems an impossible, unsustainable alternative.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_244674597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/244674597" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/244674597?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141032917" href="/book/show/1183167.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pernod-and-gauloises">pernod-and-gauloises</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=distaff">distaff</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novellas">novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer244674597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16788486833164888142">First, a digression. (How can one digress before the story has even begun? Surely for a digression to take place, a tangible thread needs to be established? Well, what is this parenthesis exactly, if not a digression? Point proven). So: that digression I promised. My first brush with love was with a Scottish lassie named Emma (not a very Scots name, but if local flavour is required, let’s call her Agnes). So Emma-Agnes was the victim of my affections and the entire “passionate” encounter is best</span>
  <span id="freeText16788486833164888142" style="display:none">First, a digression. (How can one digress before the story has even begun? Surely for a digression to take place, a tangible thread needs to be established? Well, what is this parenthesis exactly, if not a digression? Point proven). So: that digression I promised. My first brush with love was with a Scottish lassie named Emma (not a very Scots name, but if local flavour is required, let’s call her Agnes). So Emma-Agnes was the victim of my affections and the entire “passionate” encounter is best described a “polite” encounter. In fact, excessive politeness was responsible for our inevitable separation.<br /><br />It happened thus. I had been friends with Emma-Agnes for a few years in school and decided to write a page-long summation of my feelings toward her, apologising for my inappropriate biological urges impeding on our friendship. I expressed regret that I was attracted to her, and understood entirely if she’d want to sever our union and banish me, even though we took the same train daily, the same classes, and a few tutorials. To my surprise, she wasn’t repulsed and we carried on as friends. A few months later I wrote a second letter asking if we might go to lunch together, if that wasn’t too forward, and I would pay for her meal, if that wasn’t too sexist an attitude to take. She agreed.<br /><br />And it progressed at this pace over the year. I eventually wrote her a letter requesting a lip-to-lip exchange, which occurred a month after the letter had been sent. Emma-Agnes already had a boyfriend at this stage, and would fall pregnant a few months later, but she kept up her side of the agreement. On an empty train carriage, I leaned in for the exchange. I hovered close to her face, then stopped to ask her if this was the correct angle for a satisfying “kiss.” She nodded and egged me on cordially. There was contact: her lips were a little sticky from lipgloss, so it was like kissing a Jelly Baby’s innards. After the peck, I was on the point of collapse. She was offering a second, more fuller exchange, but I decided that was enough for one afternoon. Absolutely marvellous. (You may baulk, but we shy people take what we get in this life, and when we love, we love like dying men crying out for morphine).<br /><br />She left to have her baby a few months later and I didn’t see her again. It seemed she preferred the father of her child to me. I guess he was a little more assertive a lover. Ah well, the delirium of young love! This book is good.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16788486833164888142" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
