<div id="review_1308483463" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1308483463" />
    <a title="shakespeareandspice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2576681-shakespeareandspice"><img alt="shakespeareandspice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497035907p2/2576681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1308483463?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="shakespeareandspice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="shakespeareandspice" href="/user/show/2576681-shakespeareandspice">shakespeareandspice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780241951569" href="/book/show/290897.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576681-shakespeareandspice?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576681-shakespeareandspice?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2576681-shakespeareandspice?shelf=own-in-print">own-in-print</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1308483463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13393683492546799790"><i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> is one of those books that, while it’s quick and easy to read, generates a lot of contemplation.<br/><br/>Cécile, our narrator, is perhaps one of the most unlikable characters I’ve ever read. She is a seventeen-year-old overindulgent, pampered child of a father who lives in his own life rather carefree. One summer vacation, Cécile’s life is interrupted by a blast from their past and things take a turn for the worse. It doesn’t help that this story is recounted by her adult self and the t</span>
  <span id="freeText13393683492546799790" style="display:none"><i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> is one of those books that, while it’s quick and easy to read, generates a lot of contemplation.<br /><br />Cécile, our narrator, is perhaps one of the most unlikable characters I’ve ever read. She is a seventeen-year-old overindulgent, pampered child of a father who lives in his own life rather carefree. One summer vacation, Cécile’s life is interrupted by a blast from their past and things take a turn for the worse. It doesn’t help that this story is recounted by her adult self and the tone she takes clearly lacks of any empathy towards the troublesome decisions she’s made in her younger years.<br /><br />Though Cécile’s unchecked attitude causes a lot of trouble in the story, the blame is to be equally placed with her father, Raymond. While he’s a quiet figure and doesn’t often get a voice—certainly not as much as the women, even Elsa and Anne—the way he’s raised Cécile is deeply problematic. He seems to dangle back and forth between what what makes everyone happy and what would be the right thing given the circumstance. Neither need be exclusive but at times they are.<br /><br />The writing style works well with the narrative of the book. The fact that this story is actually told in flashback adds more to Cécile’s character and gives us a chance to observe the aftereffects of the events which take place in the book. It equally adds to our aversion of Cécile but at the same time, also emphasizes the negative consequences of being too frivolous with one’s decisions.<br /><br />The ending is a bit problematic for me because it felt unnecessary to deal with the conflict between Anne and Cécile this way. I also had to question the morality of Cécile and Raymond with how irreverently they seemed to have dealt with the situation at the end. This ties back in to how the adult Cécile is looking back on her younger years and still doesn’t seem to think much of how much her actions has cost others.<br /><br />Not a book meant for everyone but a satisfying read if you are comfortable with unlikable characters. It lays somewhere between a two- and three-star read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13393683492546799790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duane" href="/user/show/24389390-duane">Duane</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061440793" href="/book/show/3223882-bonjour-tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=2016-book-challenge">2016-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=reviewed-books">reviewed-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=rated-books">rated-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24389390-duane?shelf=guardian-1000">guardian-1000</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1616031625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9885282105588275155">This review may contain spoilers.<br/><br/>Franchise Sagan was 17 when she wrote this novel, which is yet another example of a teen writer finding their way into the realm of classic world literature. Published in 1954, it had immediate success in France and a year later in the US, and 60 years later is still being enjoyed by readers everywhere. I think people were astonished first by the quality of the writing, and second by the level of insight the young writer had regarding serious adult relationships </span>
  <span id="freeText9885282105588275155" style="display:none">This review may contain spoilers.<br /><br />Franchise Sagan was 17 when she wrote this novel, which is yet another example of a teen writer finding their way into the realm of classic world literature. Published in 1954, it had immediate success in France and a year later in the US, and 60 years later is still being enjoyed by readers everywhere. I think people were astonished first by the quality of the writing, and second by the level of insight the young writer had regarding serious adult relationships and situations. <br /><br />It's the story of 17 year old Cecile and her playboy father Raymond (a widower), and their amoral view of life and love, as they lay waste to the emotions of everyone they come into contact with. It's a tragic story really, but you can't help but smile at the precociousness of this young character.<br /><br />3.5 stars</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8604736307946864023" style="display:none">"Goodbye Sadness." I was drawn to the screen adaptation of this book years ago because of the title and I really enjoyed it. <div id="review_3017145160" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3017145160" />
    <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic"><img alt="Ivana Books Are Magic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578249636p2/56173179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3017145160?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivana Books Are Magic" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic">Ivana Books Are Magic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3017145160" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13843924271676956805">The protagonist of the novel is a young girl named Cecile, who doesn’t want her widowed father to remarry because well she likes her life the way it is. Her father is a womanizer, but she doesn’t mind it, presumably because that is what makes her the emotional centre of his life. Anyhow, it is the life she is used to and we humans in general are not very keen on change, are we? Bonjour Tristesse is not a long novel, I would say it is something you can read in two hours or less. So, if you’re loo</span>
  <span id="freeText13843924271676956805" style="display:none">The protagonist of the novel is a young girl named Cecile, who doesn’t want her widowed father to remarry because well she likes her life the way it is. Her father is a womanizer, but she doesn’t mind it, presumably because that is what makes her the emotional centre of his life. Anyhow, it is the life she is used to and we humans in general are not very keen on change, are we? Bonjour Tristesse is not a long novel, I would say it is something you can read in two hours or less. So, if you’re looking for a shorter novel, this might do the trick. If you want to read something that is not too complicated or emotionally draining, but is at the same time original and interesting, this might be a good novel for you.<br /><br />Despite its instant popularity, the novel itself was met with mixed review, some critics praised this novel while others thought it became popular only because of the author’s age. I can't help but wonder how this novel would be received if it was published today. Would it still be considered controversial? Bonjour Tristesse caused a fair deal of controversy in its time. Some people still see it as a critique of traditional values but I’m not sure would I go that far. It is certainly a fascinating book. I would agree that it was, in many ways, ahead of its time, but I don’t think it is Sagan’s best work. Basically, my thoughts on it amount to this- This is a great novel to get yourself introduced with Sagan’s works, but if you’re planning to read only one novel of hers, perhaps this is not the best one to choose. In other words, this is a very good novel but it is not her best because it lacks maturity (hardly surprising since she was 17 when she wrote it). <br /><br />“A Strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sorrow. The idea of sorrow has always appealed to me but now I am almost ashamed of its complete egoism. I have known boredom, regret, and occasionally remorse, but never sorrow. Today it envelops me like a silken web, enervating and soft, and sets me apart from everybody else.” <br /><br /><br />A lot of people seem to either love or hate this book. I don’t fall into either category. I quite liked this novel, I thought it was quite unique. However, I must admit that I found it to be somewhat lacking. A few years back when I wrote my first review of it on goodreads, I said that I liked this novel but that it lacked depth. After some thinking, I came to conclusion that I was wrong when I initially called this novel a light read. It frustrated me a little (at times), but is surely not a novel that completely lacks depth. Perhaps it is not as complex as I would like it to be, but how complex one can be at seventeen? So, it might not be as profound as I expected but it is still an interesting read. <br /><br />What this novel lacks in maturity, it makes up in being genuine. It seems to capture that freshness of youth and even manages to contrast it with another important feature of youth (one is often ignored) and that is a feeling of isolation. Moreover, it conveys the intensity of emotion in adolescence quite well. Being a teenager often means feeling like the world is going to end with every twist of your heart, but as you grow older you see there is more to life than this emotional role coaster. This novel is very good at capturing that spirit of confused youth, very good at understanding how the selfishness of youth often has its own logic. The fact that the narrator of this novel really feels like a teenager is not surprising, because the novel was written by a teenager. The narrative is quite convincing and while reading you have a feeling like you’re inside this young girl’s head. I think this novel is a good example why writing at a young age makes sense. Even if your work isn’t perfect, it will probably feel and be authentic- you’d be too young to know how to fake things. All things considering, I think the author (and the publishers) did the right thing by publishing this novel. In a way, this novel is even more convincing because it was written by someone that young. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13843924271676956805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_77770595" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77770595" />
    <a title="Kasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1504904-kasia"><img alt="Kasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308041294p2/1504904.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/77770595?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kasia" href="/user/show/1504904-kasia">Kasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1504904-kasia?shelf=je-ne-comprend-pas">je-ne-comprend-pas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1504904-kasia?shelf=lovely-covers">lovely-covers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1504904-kasia?shelf=neurotic-literary-snoot">neurotic-literary-snoot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1504904-kasia?shelf=1001-done-with">1001-done-with</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77770595" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12964556082216230243">"Bonjou, Tristesse" or "Hello, Sadness", is not as sad or depressing as the title suggests. There's no debate whether you should I slash your wrists or put a bullet in your cranium. Nothing like that. It's French riviera, adultery, streaming sun and first kisses. All followed by a cynical, but necessary in my opinion, ending.<br/><br/>The book is mighty short mind you, and the writing surprisingly mature. Sagan was 18 this was published, did you know? I didn't (not until I was done with the book). <br/><br/>In all </span>
  <span id="freeText12964556082216230243" style="display:none">"Bonjou, Tristesse" or "Hello, Sadness", is not as sad or depressing as the title suggests. There's no debate whether you should I slash your wrists or put a bullet in your cranium. Nothing like that. It's French riviera, adultery, streaming sun and first kisses. All followed by a cynical, but necessary in my opinion, ending.<br /><br />The book is mighty short mind you, and the writing surprisingly mature. Sagan was 18 this was published, did you know? I didn't (not until I was done with the book). <br /><br />In all my ignorance, I was under the impression that this was written by an old Nabokov like creep. The lovely detailed descriptions of Côte d'Azur (I almost felt sand in my hair and sun on my face as I read it, and it's snowing over here now), the acute portrayal of a 17yo spoiled girl (in this aspect, I believe Sagan's age was mightily helpful), the in depth analysis of peoples motives and behaviors - that's what must have led me astray. The tantrums, the hatred, the constant scheming, the egoism, the lies, the father worship, the adultery, the avoidance - it was all pointing out to a well aged male writer, not to a young debutante. And the writing was superb, did I mention it yet? Well, ok, dialogs excluded, those felt tad unnatural and lengthy, in my experience simple constructs and short sentences work best in speech, but I'm prepared to blame the nameless translator for that. When in doubt always blame the translator. That's my rule. <br /><br />Apart from the exquisite imagery, the main strength of <i>Bonjou, Tristesse</i> is the seventeen-year-old Cécile. She considers herself superior, sophisticated and oh so mature. Which, of course, entitles her to meddling in other people's affairs, taking lovers (twice her age would be most appropriate) if only she wishes, and disregarding any advise. Cécile is snooty, temperamental and a typical teenager. But most importantly so much more real that the idolized Bella (for those of you who are keeping up with the Twilight extravaganza).<br /><br />So why the so-so rating, would you ask. Well, first of all, it's not my favorite book. Tad snooty and never too cheerful with that inherently French ideas of love and adultery, it was hard for me to like. Besiedes, I appreciated what it stood for, I appreciated the talent behind it, but I enjoyed only parts of it. Yet, even though I did not quite love it, I would still recommend it to those with a preference for literary fiction - it's a nice French gem (an overnight sensation back when it was first published) that should not be forgotten. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12964556082216230243" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2248772159" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2248772159" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2248772159?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2248772159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7975593505382376225">"Mes jours comme mes nuits sont en tous points pareils, sans joies et pleins d'ennui..." <br/><br/>Françoise Hardy's words are quite fitting when it comes to describing the languorous atmosphere of "Bonjour Tristesse", another Françoise's first novel. An important novel, in spite of its flaws and shortcomings: one of those books a whole society must cope with once in a decade. <br/><br/>F. Sagan is only eighteen when she writes about Cécile, seventeen, a pampered teenage Parisian living with a pampered young father</span>
  <span id="freeText7975593505382376225" style="display:none">"Mes jours comme mes nuits sont en tous points pareils, sans joies et pleins d'ennui..." <br /><br />Françoise Hardy's words are quite fitting when it comes to describing the languorous atmosphere of "Bonjour Tristesse", another Françoise's first novel. An important novel, in spite of its flaws and shortcomings: one of those books a whole society must cope with once in a decade. <br /><br />F. Sagan is only eighteen when she writes about Cécile, seventeen, a pampered teenage Parisian living with a pampered young father, Raymond. A life that so far has been pretty comfortable for both: wealthy, shallow, free; no pressure, no expectations; daddy's volatile relationships are a funny pastime in which Cécile is a willing accomplice. Until summer comes.<br /><br />A hot summer on the Côte d'Azur. The sea, the blinding sun, a house on the beach... then father and daughter are joined by Anne, an acquaintance from Paris, a woman whose charm and intelligence are definitely new to both of them. <br />The troubles begin when Raymond suddenly falls in love with Anne, who expects him to behave as a man and straighten up once and for all. The sight of her father utterly spellbound by the prospect of growing up horrifies Cécile, whose feelings for Anne are painfully in contrast with her frivolous attitude toward life. She's strangely attracted to this woman - an attraction that often gets almost physical - fascinated and bewildered by her uniqueness; at the same time she senses all the danger Anne represents, how radically she's going to change their life. Anne is not one of the cheerful morons her father picks up wantonly, forever in need to feel young and attractive; this woman is a threat to their life of ease and irresponsibility. <br />Naive and merciless, childish and cold blooded, what Cécile sets in motion is a cruel game in which everyone gets involved: Raymond, his ex girlfriend, Cécile summer boyfriend. Tragedy will inexorably seal the last summer of Cécile's innocence. <br /><br />Sagan is neither Yourcenar nor Beauvoir. She's not Duras... nor Arsan, if anything. She's a teenager who writes about another teenager, basically describing her own life and the adult world as she sees it: the glamourous milieu of wealthy post-war France. Hedonism has prevailed over any sort of intellectual or political commitment, let aside the leftist intelligentsia of the 50s and 60s - already turning into a caricature of itself when Sagan writes her novel. It's 1954: Algeria is getting out of control, the situation in Vietnam is falling apart, but nobody really cares as long as De Gaulle says it's all right. <br />Her being far from any intellectual commitment is precisely what makes her work so powerful and momentous. It takes a girl of 18 to talk about the sexual acts and thoughts of young people, a revelation that sounds more spontaneous and genuine when it doesn't come from an old lady remembering her pre-war high times (Duras) or a diplomat's wife bored to death (Arsan). <br />It's a modern counterpoint to "Les Liaisons Dangereuses". Same coldness, same ruthlessness in planning the destruction of other people's hopes and expectations. Cécile is a puppeteer, the deus ex machina of the game, until she realises that nobody - not even her - really knows how to play it. <br />"Bonjour Tristesse" (the title comes from a poem by Paul Éluard) is still a scandalous book. It's a book on cruelty: the cruelty of innocence and the innocence of cruelty. <br /><br />Sagan's talent burnt itself soon after the enormous success of her first novel. The unexpected celebrity, the greed of publishers and readers, the expectations, the attention of intellectuals and ideologues (not to mention drugs, alcohol, failed relationships, a major car crash...) destroyed her potential from the very beginning. <br />"Bonjour Tristesse" is the spiritual testament of a generation of wasted intelligence and talent, gone sour on a path of easy money and excess. <br />The Mediterranean waves of the Côte d'Azur echo with a sadness we don't really feel like greeting with a Bonjour. <br /><br />Bonjour à toi, Françoise... où que tu sois. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7975593505382376225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2248772159">

<div id="review_152430037" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/152430037" />
    <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly"><img alt="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349701607p2/2190636.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/152430037?book_show_action=true">Mar 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly" href="/user/show/2190636-joselito-honestly-and-brilliantly">Joselito Honestly and Brilliantly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer152430037" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16367881978102307032">This caused a sensation when it was first published in 1959. Why? The author was just 18 years old when she wrote this and the principal protagonist was a 17-year-old girl, with her 40-year-old wealthy father, both carefree and hedonistic. The father treats his sexual conquests like sporting events; the daughter (who narrates the story), in turn, admits that her love of pleasure "seems to be the only coherent side of (her) character." She's "vaguely uncomfortable with anyone devoid of physical c</span>
  <span id="freeText16367881978102307032" style="display:none">This caused a sensation when it was first published in 1959. Why? The author was just 18 years old when she wrote this and the principal protagonist was a 17-year-old girl, with her 40-year-old wealthy father, both carefree and hedonistic. The father treats his sexual conquests like sporting events; the daughter (who narrates the story), in turn, admits that her love of pleasure "seems to be the only coherent side of (her) character." She's "vaguely uncomfortable with anyone devoid of physical charms" and the only thing she describes as somehow "indecent" (even with all her father's amorous escapades in full view), are those plain-looking people who are "resigned to the fact that they are unattractive."<br /><br />Think Paris Hilton (or maybe Lindsay Lohan?) with anyone of her parents. The famously rich surrounded by beauty. The only difference, perhaps, is that while Francoise Sagan's heroine (or Sagan herself) showed a lot of promise and literary sophistication at such a young age (the beach, she's sunbathing, her lover approaches her, she hears his voice, but she could not speak because, as she magnificently says, she "was nailed to the sand by all the forces of summer"), her modern day versions could hardly write one complete paragraph without being objects of derision.<br /><br />A nice little pocketbook of only about a hundred pages. Quite a disgusting accomplishment! Only wimps read cute little books like this! To regain my self-respect, I shall now face the ten demons in my closet, books in the 1001 list which I have not read and with more than 500 pages each:<br /><br />1. A TALE OF LOVE AND DARKNESS -Amos Oz, 517 pages;<br />2. LANARK: A LIFE IN FOUR BOOKS - Alasdair Gray, 573 pages;<br />3. THE COMPLETE STORIES OF SAKI - Hector Hugh Munro, 594 pages;<br />4. LOST ILLUSIONS - Honore de Balsac, 641 pages;<br />5. GREEN HENRY - Gottfried Keller, 706 pages;<br />6. THE FORSYTE SAGA - John Galsworthy, 715 pages;<br />7. IN THE FIRST CIRCLE - Alexandr Solzhenitsyn, 741 pages;<br />8. PARADE'S END - Ford Madox Ford, 836 pages;<br />9. BELLE DU SEIGNEUR - Albert Cohen, 974 pages; <br /><br />and Satan himself:<br /><br />10. AGAINST THE DAY - Thomas Pynchon, 1220 pages.<br /><br /> 7,517 pages. If I read 30 pages a day, I'll finish all these in about 8 months. Only then can I have a really merry christmas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16367881978102307032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2231288065" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2231288065?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140278781" href="/book/show/805039.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2231288065" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17008647403304981461"><i>
  <blockquote>A strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sadness. In the past the idea of sadness always appealed to me, now I am almost ashamed of its complete egoism. </blockquote>
</i><br/>How strikingly different this is from the literature of 1950s England: laddish books of class and ambition like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1775733.Room_at_the_Top' title='Room at the Top by John Braine' rel='nofollow noopener'>Room at the Top</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18875995.A_Kind_of_Loving' title='A Kind of Loving by Stan Barstow' rel='nofollow noopener'>A Kind of Loving</a> where young women are trophies and obstacles with their ever-present desired and frightening bodies. Sagan's narrator, in contrast, is the 17 </span>
  <span id="freeText17008647403304981461" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>A strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sadness. In the past the idea of sadness always appealed to me, now I am almost ashamed of its complete egoism. </blockquote>
</i><br />How strikingly different this is from the literature of 1950s England: laddish books of class and ambition like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1775733.Room_at_the_Top" title="Room at the Top by John Braine" rel="nofollow noopener">Room at the Top</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18875995.A_Kind_of_Loving" title="A Kind of Loving by Stan Barstow" rel="nofollow noopener">A Kind of Loving</a> where young women are trophies and obstacles with their ever-present desired and frightening bodies. Sagan's narrator, in contrast, is the 17 year old Cecile: amoral, smart, sexual and sophisticated - a whole different order from her English sisters.<br /><br />Set on the Riviera, Sagan's writing is beautiful: emotionally fluent, with a pellucid clarity, as relationships shift through various triangles, some directed ambiguously by Cecile herself - who is, ultimately, far less in control than she'd like to think herself.<br /><br />Throughout, this reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1424484.Les_Liaisons_dangereuses" title="Les Liaisons dangereuses by Pierre-Ambroise Choderlos de Laclos" rel="nofollow noopener">Les Liaisons dangereuses</a>, but a simplified, modernised version where Sagan's Cecile merges the innocent, pseudo-sophistication of Laclos' Cecile with his Marquise de Merteuil. The textual relations are light, barely hinted at, but resonant.<br /><br />At just over 100 pages, this is short in length but it feels perfectly paced: hedonistic and decadent on the surface, it hides a sensitive, melancholic heart.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17008647403304981461" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_58974092" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/58974092" />
    <a title="Evan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1248986-evan"><img alt="Evan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488574589p2/1248986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58974092?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evan" href="/user/show/1248986-evan">Evan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7797703-bonjour-tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=yearning">yearning</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=2010-reads">2010-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=bohemian-rhapsodies">bohemian-rhapsodies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1248986-evan?shelf=__in-my-collection">__in-my-collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58974092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11432860127377656656">Far lesser tragedies -- and all too often, greater ones -- than the one led up to during the ritzy summer Mediterranean vacation described in <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> occur constantly because people want to preserve a way of life or gain some advantage in love or power or materialism.<br/><br/>In this book, the young, carefree protagonist, Cecile, fears the loss of a way of life, preferring it to the uncertainty of the future. Perhaps she fears even more the loss of the true love of her life, her father. As long</span>
  <span id="freeText11432860127377656656" style="display:none">Far lesser tragedies -- and all too often, greater ones -- than the one led up to during the ritzy summer Mediterranean vacation described in <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> occur constantly because people want to preserve a way of life or gain some advantage in love or power or materialism.<br /><br />In this book, the young, carefree protagonist, Cecile, fears the loss of a way of life, preferring it to the uncertainty of the future. Perhaps she fears even more the loss of the true love of her life, her father. As long his romances are frivolous and non-committal, there is nothing to threaten this dynamic. The potential rival and Cecile's soon-to-be stepmother, Anne, is the interloper about to make good on that threat. Almost guilelessly, and half-disbelieving that her machinations could possibly work, Cecile's plans to preserve the lifestyle of her youth and nip in the bud the authoritarian, moralistic structure promised by Anne prove to be all too successful. And yet, even with Cecile's regret, there is also a relief that is downright amoral.<br /><br />This is the story of an adolescent, written by an adolescent, expressing as well as any young writer ever has the struggles and mysteries of being a young girl, of a life still to be lived, of an amorphous POV, of how one relates to other people and the world; of trying to find a balance between the responsible and the carefree -- of feeling the powers of youth and its parallel impotencies. Of making the first tentative steps into the mysteries of love. Of holding onto youth and fearing adulthood. Of wanting to be true to oneself while not being sure of oneself.<br /><br />It's exquisitely expressed and an unconditional pleasure to read.<br /><br />My Goodreads friends here who have read this and bothered to review it, and who happened to once be 17-year-old girls, have probably gotten at the essence of this better than I could have. I defer to them.<br /><br />Sagan writes about a milieu she knows, and even though her settings and attitudes are those of the idle rich, this book is far more interesting than most novels that aim to be critiques of the amorality of the wealthy -- mainly because it doesn't overly emphasize those things and because Sagan is more interested in feelings and motivations. This makes the book more universal to me.<br /><br />The character of Anne in this book strikes me as far more complicated and enigmatic than that presented by actress Deborah Kerr in the 1958 film version. The reader's feelings about her shift as frequently as Cecile's own constant reassessments.<br /><br />This book is no B.S.; no dicking around; no showing off. <br />At the end of the day and when all is said and done, and all the goofy, annoying literary experiments have been tried and foisted upon me, I point to Francoise Sagan -- what she did in this debut novel and her second one (<i>A Certain Smile</i>) -- as prime examples of what good, thoughtful writing really is, to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11432860127377656656" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1728909657" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1728909657" />
    <a title="Claire" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2528841-claire"><img alt="Claire" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1336501182p2/2528841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1728909657?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire" href="/user/show/2528841-claire">Claire</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780241951569" href="/book/show/290897.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=around-the-world-2016">around-the-world-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=women-in-translation">women-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=around-the-world">around-the-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2528841-claire?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1728909657" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10844570715417195944">Rachel Cooke in this Guardian article <a href='https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/jul/24/subtle-art-of-translating-foreign-fiction-ferrante-knausgaard' rel='nofollow noopener'>The subtle art of translation</a> reflects on the importance of the right translation and relates her memory of reading Bonjour Tristesse.<br/><blockquote>a novel I have loved ever since I first read it as a teenager, and whose dreamy opening line in its original translation from the French by Irene Ash – “A strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sadness” – I know by heart.</blockquote><br/>She decides to splash out and buy a new copy to read an</span>
  <span id="freeText10844570715417195944" style="display:none">Rachel Cooke in this Guardian article <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/jul/24/subtle-art-of-translating-foreign-fiction-ferrante-knausgaard" rel="nofollow noopener">The subtle art of translation</a> reflects on the importance of the right translation and relates her memory of reading Bonjour Tristesse.<br /><blockquote>a novel I have loved ever since I first read it as a teenager, and whose dreamy opening line in its original translation from the French by Irene Ash – “A strange melancholy pervades me to which I hesitate to give the grave and beautiful name of sadness” – I know by heart.</blockquote><br />She decides to splash out and buy a new copy to read and chooses the Penguin Modern Classics version translated by Heather Lloyd.<br /><blockquote>Some days later, in bed, I began reading it. The shock was tremendous, disorienting. “This strange new feeling of mine, obsessing me by its sweet languor, is such that I am reluctant to dignify it with the fine, solemn name of ‘sadness’,” went the first sentence, which sounded to my ears a little as though a robot had written it.</blockquote><br />For a while she continued to read it, telling herself it was stupid to cling to one version, as if it were a sacred thing, however she gave up, it may have been an accurate translation but it lacked the magic of that fist reading experience. She ends by saying that if you tried this story and hated it, to please have another go and entrust yourself to Irene Ash's gorgeous 1955 translation.<br /><br />Having read that I had no hesitation in going straight for the Irene Ash translation and was transfixed from the very first pages, totally put under the spell of this charming little novella.<br /><br />Cecile is looking back and recalling the summer she was seventeen, when she and her father spent 2 months on the French Riveria near St Raphael, having a blissful holiday. He is a widower who doesn't lack for female company and she has just finished school and lives a life of privilege and indulgence, her father imposing few if any limits on her, they are in a sense like children both of them in adult bodies.<br /><br />Cecile is surprised to enjoy the company of a young man Cyril, preferring the company of more mature men and her father's friends, and discovers she quite likes the attentions of this young man who is falling in love with her and she with him.<br /><br />It should have been a perfect summer, but things change when an old friend of her mother's, Anne arrives and she and her father announce their intention to marry. Although it is actually something Cecile feels is right for them and she adores Anne, part of her resents what signifies to her the end to the playful era she and her father have indulged, for Anne's presence in their lives will certainly bring order and sensibility. <br /><blockquote>Yes, it was for this I reproached Anne: she prevented me from liking myself. I, who was so naturally meant for happiness and gaiety, had been forced by her into a world of self-criticism and guilty conscience, where, unaccustomed to introspection, I was completely lost. And what did she bring me? I took stock: She wanted my father, she had got him. She would gradually make of us the husband and step-daughter of Anne Larsen; that is to say, she would turn us into two civilised, well-behaved and happy people...<br /><br />Tears came into my eyes at the thought of the jokes we used to have together, our laughter as we drove home at dawn through the empty streets of Paris. All that was over. In my turn I would be influenced, re-oriented, remodelled by Anne. I would not even mind it, she would act with intelligence, irony and sweetness, and I would be incapable of resistance; in six months I should no longer even wish to resist.</blockquote><br /><br />She embarks on a plan to provoke a change in this happy little situation, instantly regretting it, but unable to halt the progress of a development she has initiated.<br /><br />It is a simple storyline, but what makes it incredible are the adept insights Cecile has into herself and her behaviour and to all those around her. She acts irresponsibly as if she is unable to help herself, but with a certain equanimity, it is as if she stands outside of herself and narrates events and what is driving each character to act their part in her little drama, which will escalate into tragedy.<br /><br />Utterly engaging, I was riveted, loved that ability her character had to understand the personalities around her and her own flaws, despite being unable to stop the mischief she provoked, not to mention that this was written when the author was only 19 years old.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10844570715417195944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1737576463" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1737576463" />
    <a title="Michelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57178492-michelle"><img alt="Michelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534028025p2/57178492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1737576463?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michelle" href="/user/show/57178492-michelle">Michelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57178492-michelle?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57178492-michelle?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1737576463" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer545169222991644899"><p>Cécile is seventeen and perfectly happy. She is spending the summer with her father, Raymond, a wealthy forty-year-old widower and his mistress, Elsa, an attractive twenty-something redhead who earns her living by posing for artists while flitting from one sugar daddy to another. On a whim, Raymond invites Anne, a friend of Cécile’s deceased mother, to join them at their rented villa. The ensuing <i>ménage-à-quatre</i> is untenable—jealousies and power struggles arise. Cécile cannot bear for anyone to </p></span>
  <span id="freeText545169222991644899" style="display:none"><p>Cécile is seventeen and perfectly happy. She is spending the summer with her father, Raymond, a wealthy forty-year-old widower and his mistress, Elsa, an attractive twenty-something redhead who earns her living by posing for artists while flitting from one sugar daddy to another. On a whim, Raymond invites Anne, a friend of Cécile’s deceased mother, to join them at their rented villa. The ensuing <i>ménage-à-quatre</i> is untenable—jealousies and power struggles arise. Cécile cannot bear for anyone to disrupt the free and easy life she has with her father; she is hostile toward Anne and devises a plot to push her out of their lives forever.<br /></p><p>I read <i>Aimez-vous Brahms</i> earlier this year, making this my second Françoise Sagan novel. Both novels feature bourgeois characters who have everything money can buy, yet their lives are empty. They yearn for true love and connection, but have no idea how to attain it. They are lazy and listless, chasing after sensual pleasures whether in sex, alcohol, or driving fast cars. They are exquisitely bored, and I felt exquisitely bored reading about them.<br /></p><p><i>Bonjour Tristesse</i> is Sagan’s best-known novel and has a cult following. It was published in 1954, when the author was only eighteen years old. I wish I could say that I liked it more, but I found it quite empty, although Sagan’s writing has moments of beauty, especially when she describes the quality of the sunlight and physical sensations, like the scene in which Cécile sits on the front steps of the villa with a cup of black coffee and an orange, first biting into the orange, its sweet juice flowing into her mouth, then sipping the hot bitter coffee, while the morning sun warms her hair and smooths away the bedsheet marks on her skin.<br /></p><blockquote><i>“… je m’installai tranquillement sur une marche avec une tasse de café et une orange et entamai les délices du matin : je mordais l’orange, un jus sucré giclait dans ma bouche ; une gorgée de café noir brûlant, aussitôt, et à nouveau la fraîcheur du fruit. Le soleil du matin me chauffait les cheveux, déplissait sur ma peau les marques du drap. Dans cinq minutes, j’irais me baigner.”</i> (p. 30)</blockquote><br /><p>Pretty prose for an eighteen-year-old author, but not much substance. By the end, I was glad to say <i>adieu</i> to <i>Bonjour Tristesse.</i><br /></p><p>Note: I read this novel in the original French.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="545169222991644899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2671228" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2671228" />
    <a title="Leah Williams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/165114-leah-williams"><img alt="Leah Williams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596534455p2/165114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2671228?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah Williams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah Williams" href="/user/show/165114-leah-williams">Leah Williams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780066211695" href="/book/show/828023.Bonjour_Tristesse">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">francophiles, teenagers, escapists </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/165114-leah-williams?shelf=childhood-books">childhood-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2671228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8083494396877754091">This book will always remind me of the time I read it--I think I was about 14, and it was far and away the most grown up thing I'd read. The style of the prose is very intimate; as such, the main character is very easy to relate to. Sagan does an excellent job transporting you to a very different time and place. This is a quick, enjoyable novella that is great for an airplane ride or a light summery read. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('UgpK4DsXk1vXuFP5srbASn3oMVKIKcR64876tPc8YFAc4i1V0gnL0PVIVhsFda/RwTwLrd4B5k8dcNAleJz8Kw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Xt5JFZllL0TefC6D6oxY/cE46vZlUfcaPZn9lhTUxs0QNi6gcHt3z/yMK2FdTzdmfezQCTN51S/DJ9cHm3Ratg==')})
  }

Dans ce monde où les pianos sont des mélangeurs à cocktails, la réalité semble ne pas avoir de prise. On se marie à l\'église comme on <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14137310434065176393\" style=\"display:none\">Chick, Alise, Chloé et Colin passent leur temps à dire des choses rigolotes, à écouter Duke Ellington et à patiner. Dans ce monde où les pianos sont des mélangeurs à cocktails, la réalité semble ne pas avoir de prise. On se marie à l\'église comme on va à la fête foraine et on ignore le travail, qui se réduit à une usine monstrueuse faisant tache sur le paysage. Pied de nez aux conventions romanesques et à la morale commune, L\'Écume des jours est un délice verbal et un festin poétique. Jeux de mots, néologismes, décalages incongrus... Vian surenchérit sans cesse, faisant naître comme un vertige chez le lecteur hébété, qui sourit quand il peut. Mais le véritable malaise vient d\'ailleurs : ces adolescents éternels à la sensibilité exacerbée constituent des victimes de choix. L\'obsession consumériste de Chick, née d\'une idolâtrie frénétique pour un certain Jean-Sol Partre, semble vouloir dire que le bonheur ne saurait durer. En effet, l\'asphyxie gagne du terrain, et l\'on assiste avec effroi au rétrécissement inexorable des appartements. On en veut presque à Vian d\'être aussi lucide et de ne pas s\'être contenté d\'une expérience ludique sur fond de roman d\'amour. --Sana Tang-Léopold Wauters<\/span>\n  <a Derrière les apparences flatteuses de l’homme de lettres, se cache un prédateur, couvert par une partie du milieu littéraire. V. tente de s’arracher à l’emprise qu’il exerce sur elle, tandis qu’il s’apprête à raconter leur histoire dans un roman. The Castle of Pictures present<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText12717850894395811821\" style=\"display:none\">   In her sixties, George Sand delighted in spinning tales that entertained and educated her two adored granddaughters, Aurore and Gabrielle. Fortunately, she also published thirteen of them for the rest of us to enjoy. The Castle of Pictures presents four of these stories, three of which have never before been translated into English.\n\n   Both girls and boys are depicted in these stories as empowered by curiosity, hard work, persistence, and honesty. They successfully protect themselves from danger by using their ingenuity and remaining faithful to their own consciences.\n\n   In the title story a girl becomes an artist through the persistent nurturance of her own talent despite opposition from her father, himself a painter. &quot;What Flowers Say&quot; is a wickedly funny satire of class snobbery as played out among chrysanthemums, poppies, numerous varieties of roses, and other denizens of the garden. &quot;The Bug-Eyed Fairy&quot; investigates wonders of the insect world invisible to the normal human eye. In &quot;The Talking Oak&quot;, an outcast orphan boy learns to rely on hard work and a strong sense of right and wrong to make his way first through the natural world, with the help of The Talking Oak who becomes his first friend, and then through the compexities of the world of grown-ups.\n\n   Sand never talked down to her granddaughters. Her astonishingly deep knowledge of subjects ranging from botany and lepidopterology to art history, her subtle understanding of the human heart and the creative spirit, and her sense of wonder at the       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1357577.Fran_oise_Sagan">About Françoise Sagan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1357577.Fran_oise_Sagan"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1596921136p3/1357577.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1357577.Fran_oise_Sagan">Françoise Sagan
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
971
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9254825950416580813">Born Françoise Quoirez, Sagan grew up in a French Catholic, bourgeois family. She was an independent thinker and avid reader as a young girl, and upon failing her examinations for continuing at the Sorbonne, she became a writer. <br/><br/>She went to her family's home in the south of France and wrote her first novel, <i> Bonjour Tristesse</i>, at age 18. She submitted it to Editions Juillard in January 1954 and it</span>
  <span id="freeText9254825950416580813" style="display:none">Born Françoise Quoirez, Sagan grew up in a French Catholic, bourgeois family. She was an independent thinker and avid reader as a young girl, and upon failing her examinations for continuing at the Sorbonne, she became a writer. <br /><br />She went to her family's home in the south of France and wrote her first novel, <i> Bonjour Tristesse</i>, at age 18. She submitted it to Editions Juillard in January 1954 and it was published that March. Later that year, She won the Prix des Critiques for <i>Bonjour Tristesse</i>.<br /><br />She chose "Sagan" as her pen name because she liked the sound of it and also liked the reference to the Prince and Princesse de Sagan, 19th century Parisians, who are said to be the basis of some of Marcel Proust's characters. <br /><br />She was known for her love of drinking, gambling, and fast driving. Her habit of driving fast was moderated after a serious car accident in 1957 involving her Aston Martin while she was living in Milly, France. <br /><br />Sagan was twice married and divorced, and subsequently maintained several long-term lesbian relationships. First married in 1958 to Guy Schoeller, a publisher, they divorced in 1960, and she was then married to Robert James Westhoff, an American ceramicist and sculptor, from 1962 to 63. She had one son, Denis, from her second marriage.<br /><br />She won the Prix de Monaco in 1984 in recognition of all of her work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9254825950416580813" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

Today it envelops me like a silken web, enervating and soft, and sets me apart from everybody else.&rdquo;</span>

a call to SignInPromptController#get to determine if the user should see the sign in interstial.

last seen the insterstitial.  If the controller responds indicating the popup should appear, the
